<tr>
<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;"><a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Reformation" title="Template:Reformation"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Reformation" title="Template talk:Reformation"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Reformation&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Reformation</b>, also referred to as the <b>Protestant Reformation</b>, was a <a href="/wiki/Schism" title="Schism">schism</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> initiated by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> and continued by <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a>, and other early <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformers" title="Protestant Reformers">Protestant Reformers</a> in 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. It is usually considered to have started with the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a></i> by Luther in 1517 and lasting until the end of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> in 1648.</p>
<p>Although there had been significant earlier attempts to reform the Roman Catholic Church before Luther – such as those of <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Waldo" title="Peter Waldo">Peter Waldo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a> – Martin Luther is widely acknowledged to have started the Reformation with the <i>Ninety-five Theses</i>. Luther began by criticizing the sale of <a href="/wiki/Indulgences" class="mw-redirect" title="Indulgences">indulgences</a>, insisting that the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> had no authority over <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a> and that the Catholic doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Treasury_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Treasury of Merit">merits of the saints</a> had no foundation in the Bible. The Protestant position, however, would come to incorporate doctrinal changes such as a complete reliance on Scripture as a source of religious authority (<i><a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a></i>) and the belief that faith in Jesus, and not good works, is the only way to obtain God's pardon for sin (<i><a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">sola fide</a></i>). The core motivation behind these changes was <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">theological</a>, though many other factors played a part, including the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a> that eroded faith in the <a href="/wiki/Papacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Papacy">Papacy</a>, the perceived corruption of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Curia" title="Roman Curia">Roman Curia</a>, the impact of <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>, and the new learning of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> that questioned much traditional thought.</p>
<p>The initial movement within Germany diversified, and other reform impulses arose independently of Luther. The spread of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Gutenberg's</a> <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> provided the means for the rapid dissemination of religious materials in the vernacular. The largest groups were the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutherans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinists</a>. Lutheran churches were founded mostly in Germany, the Baltics and Scandinavia, while the Reformed ones were founded in Switzerland, Hungary, France, the Netherlands and Scotland. The new movement influenced the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> decisively after 1547 under <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>, although the Church of England had been <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">made independent</a> under <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> in the early 1530s.</p>
<p>There were also reformation movements throughout continental Europe known as the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a>, which gave rise to the <a href="/wiki/Anabaptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">Pietistic</a> movements. Radical Reformers, besides forming communities outside <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state sanction</a>, often employed more extreme doctrinal change, such as the rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">tenets</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">late antique</a> councils of <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicea" class="mw-redirect" title="First Council of Nicea">Nicaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Chalcedon</a>.</p>
<p>The Roman Catholic Church responded with a <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> initiated by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a>. Much work in battling <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> was done by the well-organised new order of the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuits</a>. In general, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>, with the exception of most of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, came under the influence of Protestantism. <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a> remained Roman Catholic, while <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> was a site of a fierce conflict, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>, which left it devastated.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_early_history">Origins and early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins and early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Protestantism" title="History of Protestantism">History of Protestantism</a></div>
<p>The oldest Protestant churches, such as the <a href="/wiki/Unity_of_the_Brethren" title="Unity of the Brethren">Unitas Fratrum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian Church</a>, date their origins to <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a> (John Huss) in the early 15th century. As it was led by a Bohemian noble majority, and recognised, for a time, by the Basel Compacts, the Hussite Reformation was Europe's first "<a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</a>" because the ruling magistrates supported it, unlike the "<a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a>", which the state did not support.</p>
<p>The later Protestant Churches generally date their doctrinal separation from the Roman Catholic Church to the 16th century. The Reformation began as an attempt to reform the Roman Catholic Church, by priests who opposed what they perceived as false doctrines and ecclesiastic malpractice. They especially objected to the teaching and the sale of indulgences, and the abuses thereof, and to <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">simony</a>, the selling and buying of clerical offices. The reformers saw these practices as evidence of the systemic corruption of the <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Hierarchy of the Catholic Church">Church's hierarchy</a>, which included the pope.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earlier_schisms">Earlier schisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Earlier schisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Reformation" title="Bohemian Reformation">Bohemian Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollardy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waldensians" title="Waldensians">Waldensians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arnoldists" title="Arnoldists">Arnoldists</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muttich,_Kamil_Vladislav_-_Mistr_Jan_Hus_na_hranici_v_Kostnici_1415.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Muttich%2C_Kamil_Vladislav_-_Mistr_Jan_Hus_na_hranici_v_Kostnici_1415.jpg/170px-Muttich%2C_Kamil_Vladislav_-_Mistr_Jan_Hus_na_hranici_v_Kostnici_1415.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Muttich%2C_Kamil_Vladislav_-_Mistr_Jan_Hus_na_hranici_v_Kostnici_1415.jpg/255px-Muttich%2C_Kamil_Vladislav_-_Mistr_Jan_Hus_na_hranici_v_Kostnici_1415.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e1/Muttich%2C_Kamil_Vladislav_-_Mistr_Jan_Hus_na_hranici_v_Kostnici_1415.jpg 2x" data-file-width="324" data-file-height="456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muttich,_Kamil_Vladislav_-_Mistr_Jan_Hus_na_hranici_v_Kostnici_1415.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Execution of <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a>, an important Reformation precursor, in 1415.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unrest due to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Great Schism of Western Christianity</a> (1378–1416) excited wars between princes, uprisings among the peasants, and widespread concern over corruption in the Church. New perspectives came from <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a> at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Charles_University_in_Prague" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles University in Prague">Charles University in Prague</a>. Hus objected to some of the practices of the Roman Catholic Church and wanted to return the church in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a> to early practices <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">liturgy</a> in the language of the people (i.e. Czech), having lay people receive <a href="/wiki/Communion_rite" class="mw-redirect" title="Communion rite">communion</a> in both kinds (bread <i>and</i> wine – that is, in Latin, <a href="/wiki/Utraquist" class="mw-redirect" title="Utraquist">communio sub utraque specie</a>), married priests, and eliminating <a href="/wiki/Indulgences" class="mw-redirect" title="Indulgences">indulgences</a> and the concept of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">Purgatory</a>. Some of these, like the use of local language as the lithurgic language, were approved by the pope as early as in the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hus rejected indulgences and adopted a doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">justification by grace through faith alone</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Hus did not teach justification through faith alone (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The Roman Catholic Church officially concluded this debate at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> (1414–1417) by condemning Hus, who was executed by burning despite a promise of safe-conduct.<sup id="cite_ref-ObWall54_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ObWall54-2">[2]</a></sup> Wycliffe was posthumously condemned as a heretic and his corpse exhumed and burned in 1428.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The Council of Constance confirmed and strengthened the traditional medieval conception of church and empire. The council did not address the national tensions or the theological tensions stirred up during the previous century and could not prevent <a href="/wiki/Schism_(religion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Schism (religion)">schism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hussite_Wars" title="Hussite Wars">Hussite Wars</a> in Bohemia.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Surely we can source to something better than the 1911 Enc. Brit.? (February 2014)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_IV" title="Pope Sixtus IV">Pope Sixtus IV</a> (1471–1484) established the practice of selling indulgences to be applied to the dead, thereby establishing a new stream of revenue with agents across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Patrick1231_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick1231-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a> (1492–1503) was one of the most controversial of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> popes. He was the father of seven children, including <a href="/wiki/Lucrezia_Borgia" title="Lucrezia Borgia">Lucrezia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Borgia" title="Cesare Borgia">Cesare Borgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="BBC isn't high quality for historical purposes (March 2015)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In response to papal corruption, particularly the sale of indulgences, Luther wrote <i>The Ninety-Five Theses</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Eyewitness to History series is not exactly high quality (March 2015)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Reformation_in_Germany">Early Reformation in Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Reformation in Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther,_1528_(Veste_Coburg).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29.jpg/170px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29.jpg" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29.jpg/255px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29.jpg/340px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2148" data-file-height="3400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther,_1528_(Veste_Coburg).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, shown in a portrait by <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a>, initiated the Protestant Reformation in 1517.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Reformation is often dated to 31 October 1517 in Wittenberg, <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a>, when Luther sent his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ninety-Five_Theses" class="mw-redirect" title="The Ninety-Five Theses">Ninety-Five Theses on the Power and Efficacy of Indulgences</a></i> to the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Mainz" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishop of Mainz">Archbishop of Mainz</a>. The theses debated and criticised the Church and the papacy, but concentrated upon the selling of indulgences and doctrinal policies about <a href="/wiki/Purgatory#Protestantism" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Particular_judgment#Reformation_concepts" title="Particular judgment">particular judgment</a>, and the authority of the pope. He would later in the period 1517–1521 write works on the Catholic devotion to <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>, the intercession of and devotion to the saints, the sacraments, mandatory clerical celibacy, monasticism, further on the authority of the pope, the ecclesiastical law, censure and excommunication, the role of secular rulers in religious matters, the relationship between Christianity and the law, and <a href="/wiki/Good_works" title="Good works">good works</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schofield122_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield122-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reformers made heavy use of inexpensive pamphlets as well as vernacular Bibles using the relatively new printing press, so there was swift movement of both ideas and documents.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubin270_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AtkinsonFitzgerald15_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AtkinsonFitzgerald15-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magisterial_Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Magisterial Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</a></div>
<p>Parallel to events in Germany, a movement began in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Switzerland" title="History of Switzerland">Switzerland</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_Zwingli" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulrich Zwingli">Ulrich Zwingli</a>. These two movements quickly agreed on most issues, but some unresolved differences kept them separate. Some followers of Zwingli believed that the Reformation was too conservative, and moved independently toward more radical positions, some of which survive among modern day <a href="/wiki/Anabaptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptists</a>. Other Protestant movements grew up along lines of mysticism or <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>, sometimes breaking from Rome or from the Protestants, or forming outside of the churches.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:95Thesen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/95Thesen.jpg/170px-95Thesen.jpg" width="170" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/95Thesen.jpg/255px-95Thesen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/95Thesen.jpg/340px-95Thesen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="654" data-file-height="488" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:95Thesen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Martin Luther's <a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a> placed in doubt and repudiated several of the Roman Catholic practices.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After this first stage of the Reformation, following the <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> of Luther and condemnation of the Reformation by the Pope, the work and writings of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> were influential in establishing a loose consensus among various groups in Switzerland, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Scotland" title="History of Scotland">Scotland</a>, Hungary, Germany and elsewhere.</p>
<p>The Reformation foundations engaged with <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinianism</a>; both Luther and Calvin thought along lines linked with the theological teachings of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Augustinianism of the reformers struggled against <a href="/wiki/Pelagianism" title="Pelagianism">Pelagianism</a>, a heresy that they perceived in the Roman Catholic Church. In the course of this religious upheaval, the <a href="/wiki/German_Peasants%27_War" title="German Peasants' War">German Peasants' War</a> of 1524–1525 swept through the Bavarian, <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabian</a> principalities, including the <a href="/wiki/Black_Company" title="Black Company">Black Company</a> of <a href="/wiki/Florian_Geier" class="mw-redirect" title="Florian Geier">Florian Geier</a>, a knight from <a href="/wiki/Giebelstadt" title="Giebelstadt">Giebelstadt</a> who joined the peasants in the general outrage against the Roman Catholic hierarchy. Zwinglian and Lutheran ideas had influence with preachers within the regions that the Peasants' War occurred and upon works such as the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Articles" title="Twelve Articles">Twelve Articles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Luther, however, condemned the revolt in writings such as <i><a href="/wiki/Against_the_Murderous,_Thieving_Hordes_of_Peasants" title="Against the Murderous, Thieving Hordes of Peasants">Against the Murderous, Thieving Hordes of Peasants</a></i>; Zwingli and Luther's ally <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" class="mw-redirect" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a> also did not condone the uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Some 100,000 peasants were killed by the end of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radical_Reformation">Radical Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Radical Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a></div>
<p>The Radical Reformation was the response to what was believed to be the corruption in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> and the expanding <a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial</a> <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant movement</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> and many others. Beginning in Germany and Switzerland in the 16th century, the Radical Reformation gave birth to many radical Protestant groups throughout <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. The term covers both radical reformers like <a href="/wiki/Thomas_M%C3%BCntzer" title="Thomas Müntzer">Thomas Müntzer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Karlstadt" title="Andreas Karlstadt">Andreas Karlstadt</a>, groups like the <a href="/wiki/Zwickau_prophets" title="Zwickau prophets">Zwickau prophets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anabaptists" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptists">Anabaptist</a> groups like the <a href="/wiki/Hutterite" title="Hutterite">Hutterites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">Mennonites</a>.</p>
<p>In parts of Germany, Switzerland and Austria, a majority sympathized with the Radical Reformation despite intense persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-horsch_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horsch-15">[15]</a></sup> Although the surviving proportion of the European population that rebelled against Catholic, <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zwinglian" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwinglian">Zwinglian</a> churches was small, Radical Reformers wrote profusely and the literature on the Radical Reformation is disproportionately large, partly as a result of the proliferation of the Radical Reformation teachings in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literacy">Literacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Literacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/220px-Lutherbibel.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/330px-Lutherbibel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/440px-Lutherbibel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="759" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Martin Luther's 1534 <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Bible</a> translated into German. Luther's translation influenced the development of the current Standard German.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Reformation was a triumph of literacy and the new printing press.<sup id="cite_ref-Cameron_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[a]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rubin270_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-20">[19]</a></sup> Luther's translation of the Bible into German was a decisive moment in the spread of literacy, and stimulated as well the printing and distribution of religious books and pamphlets. From 1517 onward, religious pamphlets flooded Germany and much of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Edwards_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-21">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[b]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1530, over 10,000 publications are known, with a total of ten million copies. The Reformation was thus a media revolution. Luther strengthened his attacks on Rome by depicting a "good" against "bad" church. From there, it became clear that print could be used for propaganda in the Reformation for particular agendas. Reform writers used pre-Reformation styles, clichés and stereotypes and changed items as needed for their own purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-Edwards_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-21">[20]</a></sup> Especially effective were writings in German, including Luther's translation of the Bible, his <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Small_Catechism" title="Luther's Small Catechism">Smaller Catechism</a> for parents teaching their children, and his <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Large_Catechism" title="Luther's Large Catechism">Larger Catechism</a>, for pastors.</p>
<p>Using the German vernacular they expressed the Apostles' Creed in simpler, more personal, Trinitarian language. Illustrations in the German Bible and in many tracts popularised Luther's ideas. <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a> (1472–1553), the great painter patronised by the electors of Wittenberg, was a close friend of Luther, and he illustrated Luther's theology for a popular audience. He dramatised Luther's views on the relationship between the Old and New Testaments, while remaining mindful of Luther's careful distinctions about proper and improper uses of visual imagery.<sup id="cite_ref-Weimer387_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weimer387-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_the_Reformation">Causes of the Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Causes of the Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following supply-side factors have been identified as causes of the Reformation:<sup id="cite_ref-:0_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The presence of a printing press in a city by 1500 made Protestant adoption by 1600 far more likely.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubin270_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>Protestant literature was produced at greater levels in cities where media markets were more competitive, making these cities more likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-20">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ottoman incursions decreased conflicts between Protestants and Catholics, helping the Reformation take root.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li>Greater political autonomy increased the likelihood that Protestantism would be adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubin270_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-27">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li>Where Protestant reformers enjoyed princely patronage, they were much more likely to succeed.<sup id="cite_ref-Kim_188.E2.80.93215_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kim_188.E2.80.93215-28">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>Proximity to neighbors who adopted Protestantism increased the likelihood of adopting Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-27">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cities that had higher numbers of students enrolled in heterodox universities and lower numbers enrolled in orthodox universities were more likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-Kim_188.E2.80.93215_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kim_188.E2.80.93215-28">[26]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The following demand-side factors have been identified as causes of the Reformation:<sup id="cite_ref-:0_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Cities with strong cults of saints were less likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cities where <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a> was practiced were less likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li>Regions that were poor but had great economic potential and bad political institutions were more likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>The presence of bishoprics made the adoption of Protestantism less likely.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubin270_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>The presence of monasteries made the adoption of Protestantism less likely.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_outside_Germany">Reformation outside Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reformation outside Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(July 2017)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Reformation also spread widely throughout Europe, starting with Bohemia (Czech Lands) yet before Luther and over the next few decades also other countries.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Austria">Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Austria followed the same pattern of the <a href="/wiki/German-speaking" class="mw-redirect" title="German-speaking">German-speaking</a> states within the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, and Lutheranism became the main Protestant confession among its population. <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> gained a significant following in Austria which was concentrated in the eastern half of present-day Austria, while <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> was less successful. Eventually the adoption of the Counter-Reformation reversed the trend.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Czech_Lands">Czech Lands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Czech Lands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Reformation" title="Bohemian Reformation">Bohemian Reformation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg/170px-Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg" width="170" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg/255px-Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg/340px-Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg 2x" data-file-width="366" data-file-height="573" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Hus_at_the_Stake.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jan Hus at the <a href="/wiki/Death_by_burning" title="Death by burning">stake</a>, Jena codex (c. 1500)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a> were a Christian movement in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a> following the teachings of Czech reformer <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jan_Hus">Jan Hus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Jan Hus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Czech reformer and university professor <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a> (c. 1369–1415) became the best-known representative of the Bohemian Reformation and one of the forerunners of the Protestant Reformation.</p>
<p>Jan Hus was declared heretic and executed – burned at stake – at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> in 1415 where he arrived voluntarily to defend his teachings.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hussite_movement">Hussite movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hussite movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This predominantly religious movement was propelled by social issues and strengthened Czech national awareness. In 1417, two years after the execution of Jan Hus, the Czech reformation quickly became the chief force in the country.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a> made up the vast majority of the population, forcing the Council of Basel to recognize in 1437 a system of two "religions" for the first time signing the <a href="/wiki/Compacts_of_Basel" title="Compacts of Basel">Compacts of Basel</a> for the kingdom (Catholic and Czech <a href="/wiki/Ultraquists" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultraquists">Ultraquism</a>, a Hussite movement). Bohemia later also elected two Protestant kings (<a href="/wiki/George_of_Pod%C4%9Bbrady" title="George of Poděbrady">George of Poděbrady</a>).</p>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Habsburgs" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburgs">Habsburgs</a> took control of the region, the Hussite churches were prohibited and the kingdom partially recatholicized. Even later <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> gained a substantial following, after being permitted by the Habsburgs with the continued persecution of the Czech native Hussite churches. Many Hussites thus declared themselves Lutherans .</p>
<p>Two churches with Hussite roots are now second and third biggest churches in the predominantly agnostic country: <a href="/wiki/Czech_Brethren" class="mw-redirect" title="Czech Brethren">Czech Brethren</a> (which gave origin to the international church known as the <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian Church</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovak_Hussite_Church" title="Czechoslovak Hussite Church">Czechoslovak Hussite Church</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slovakia">Slovakia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Slovakia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> gained a significant following. <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> appeared, but was less popular. Today 8.9% are Protestant, of which 5.9% are Lutheran and 1.8% are Reformed.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Switzerland" title="Reformation in Switzerland">Reformation in Switzerland</a></div>
<p>In Switzerland, the teachings of the reformers and especially those of Zwingli and Calvin had a profound effect, despite the frequent quarrels between the different branches of the Reformation.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Huldrych_Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Huldrych Zwingli">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg/170px-Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg/255px-Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg/340px-Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="712" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a> launched the Reformation in Switzerland.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Parallel to events in Germany, a movement began in the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Confederation" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss Confederation">Swiss Confederation</a> under the leadership of Huldrych Zwingli. Zwingli was a scholar and preacher who moved to <a href="/wiki/Zurich" class="mw-redirect" title="Zurich">Zurich</a> – the then-leading city state – in 1518, a year after Martin Luther began the Reformation in Germany with his <a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a>. Although the two movements agreed on many issues of theology, as the recently introduced <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> spread ideas rapidly from place to place, some unresolved differences kept them separate. Long-standing resentment between the German states and the Swiss Confederation led to heated debate over how much Zwingli owed his ideas to Lutheranism. Although <a href="/wiki/Zwinglianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwinglianism">Zwinglianism</a> does hold uncanny resemblance to Lutheranism (it even had its own equivalent of the Ninety-five Theses, called the 67 Conclusions), historians have been unable to prove that Zwingli had any contact with Luther's publications before 1520, and Zwingli himself maintained that he had prevented himself from reading them.</p>
<p>The German Prince <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Hesse" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip of Hesse">Philip of Hesse</a> saw potential in creating an alliance between Zwingli and Luther, seeing strength in a united Protestant front. A meeting was held in his castle in 1529, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Colloquy_of_Marburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Colloquy of Marburg">Colloquy of Marburg</a>, which has become infamous for its complete failure. The two men could not come to any agreement due to their disputation over one key doctrine. Although Luther preached <a href="/wiki/Consubstantiation" title="Consubstantiation">consubstantiation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> over <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantiation</a>, he believed in the spiritual presence of Christ at the Mass. Zwingli, inspired by Dutch theologian <a href="/wiki/Honius" title="Honius">Cornelius Hoen</a>, believed that the mass was only representative and memorial – Christ was not present.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> Luther became so angry that he famously carved into the meeting table in chalk <i>Hoc Est Corpus Meum</i> – a Biblical quotation from the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a> meaning 'This is my body'. Zwingli countered this saying that <i>est</i> in that context was the equivalent of the word <i>significant</i> (signifies).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some followers of Zwingli believed that the Reformation was too conservative and moved independently toward more radical positions, some of which survive among modern day <a href="/wiki/Anabaptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptists</a>. One famous incident illustrating this was when radical Zwinglians fried and ate sausages during Lent in Zurich city square by way of protest against the Church teaching of <a href="/wiki/Good_works" title="Good works">good works</a>. Other Protestant movements grew up along the lines of mysticism or humanism (<a href="/wiki/Cf." title="Cf.">cf.</a> <a href="/wiki/Desiderius_Erasmus" class="mw-redirect" title="Desiderius Erasmus">Erasmus</a>), sometimes breaking from Rome or from the Protestants, or forming outside of the churches.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Calvin">John Calvin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: John Calvin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg/170px-John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg/255px-John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg/340px-John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg 2x" data-file-width="955" data-file-height="1174" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> was one of the leading figures of the Protestant Reformation. His legacy remains in a variety of churches.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> of Luther and condemnation of the Reformation by the Pope, the work and writings of John Calvin were influential in establishing a loose consensus among various groups in Switzerland, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, Hungary, Germany and elsewhere. After the expulsion of its Bishop in 1526, and the unsuccessful attempts of the Berne reformer <a href="/wiki/William_Farel" title="William Farel">Guillaume (William) Farel</a>, Calvin was asked to use the organisational skill he had gathered as a student of law to discipline the "fallen city" of Geneva. His "Ordinances" of 1541 involved a collaboration of Church affairs with the City council and <a href="/wiki/Consistory_(Protestantism)#Reformed_usage" title="Consistory (Protestantism)">consistory</a> to bring morality to all areas of life. After the establishment of the Geneva academy in 1559, Geneva became the unofficial capital of the Protestant movement, providing refuge for Protestant exiles from all over Europe and educating them as Calvinist missionaries. These missionaries dispersed Calvinism widely, and formed the French <a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a> in Calvin's own lifetime, as well as causing the conversion of Scotland under the leadership of the cantankerous <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> in 1560. The faith continued to spread after Calvin's death in 1563 and reached as far as Constantinople by the start of the 17th century.</p>
<p>The Reformation foundations engaged with <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinianism</a>. Both Luther and Calvin thought along lines linked with the theological teachings of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>. The Augustinianism of the Reformers struggled against <a href="/wiki/Pelagianism" title="Pelagianism">Pelagianism</a>, a heresy that they perceived in the Roman Catholic Church of their day. Unfortunately, since Calvin and Luther disagreed strongly on certain matters of theology (such as double-predestination and Holy Communion), the relationship between Lutherans and Calvinists was one of conflict.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nordic_countries">Nordic countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nordic countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Denmark-Norway_and_Holstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformation in Denmark-Norway and Holstein">Reformation in Denmark-Norway and Holstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Iceland#The_Reformation" title="Religion in Iceland">Reformation in Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Norway#From_Reformation_to_1964" title="Religion in Norway">Reformation in Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Sweden#Lutheran_Reformation" title="Religion in Sweden">Reformation in Sweden</a></i></dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_(I)_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Lucas_Cranach_%28I%29_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg/170px-Lucas_Cranach_%28I%29_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg" width="170" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Lucas_Cranach_%28I%29_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg/255px-Lucas_Cranach_%28I%29_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Lucas_Cranach_%28I%29_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg/340px-Lucas_Cranach_%28I%29_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2134" data-file-height="3253" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_(I)_-_Johannes_Bugenhagen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bugenhagen" title="Johannes Bugenhagen">Johannes Bugenhagen</a> introduced Protestantism in Denmark.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> ultimately adopted Lutheranism over the course of the 16th century, as the monarchs of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (who also ruled <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (who also ruled <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>) converted to that faith.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Sweden, the Reformation was spearheaded by <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Vasa" class="mw-redirect" title="Gustav Vasa">Gustav Vasa</a>, elected king in 1523. Friction with the pope over the latter's interference in Swedish ecclesiastical affairs led to the discontinuance of any official connection between Sweden and the papacy from 1523. Four years later, at the Diet of Västerås, the king succeeded in forcing the diet to accept his dominion over the national church. The king was given possession of all church property, church appointments required royal approval, the clergy were subject to the civil law, and the "pure Word of God" was to be preached in the churches and taught in the schools – effectively granting official sanction to Lutheran ideas.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I_of_Denmark" title="Frederick I of Denmark">Frederick I</a> (1523–33), Denmark remained officially Roman Catholic. But though Frederick initially pledged to persecute Lutherans, he soon adopted a policy of protecting Lutheran preachers and reformers, of whom the most famous was <a href="/wiki/Hans_Tausen" title="Hans Tausen">Hans Tausen</a>. During his reign, Lutheranism made significant inroads among the Danish population. Frederick's son, Christian, was openly Lutheran, which prevented his election to the throne upon his father's death. In 1536, the authority of the Roman Catholic bishops was terminated by national assembly. The next year, following his victory in the <a href="/wiki/Count%27s_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Count's War">Count's War</a>, he became king as <a href="/wiki/Christian_III_of_Denmark" title="Christian III of Denmark">Christian III</a> and continued the <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Denmark-Norway_and_Holstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformation in Denmark-Norway and Holstein">Reformation of the state church</a> with assistance of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bugenhagen" title="Johannes Bugenhagen">Johannes Bugenhagen</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iceland">Iceland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Iceland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Reformation" title="Icelandic Reformation">Icelandic Reformation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Luther</a>'s influence had already reached <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> before King Christian's decree. The <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">Germans</a> fished near Iceland's coast, and the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> engaged in commerce with the Icelanders. These Germans raised a Lutheran church in <a href="/wiki/Hafnarfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Hafnarfjörður">Hafnarfjörður</a> as early as 1533. Through German trade connections, many young <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelanders</a> studied in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> In 1538, when the kingly decree of the new Church ordinance reached Iceland, bishop <a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%96gmundur&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ögmundur (page does not exist)">Ögmundur</a> and his clergy denounced it, threatening excommunication for anyone subscribing to the German 'heresy'.<sup id="cite_ref-J.C3.B3n_R._Hj.C3.A1lmarsson_1993_p._70_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J.C3.B3n_R._Hj.C3.A1lmarsson_1993_p._70-35">[33]</a></sup> In 1539, the King sent a new governor to Iceland, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Klaus_von_Mervitz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Klaus von Mervitz (page does not exist)">Klaus von Mervitz</a>, with a mandate to introduce reform and take possession of church property.<sup id="cite_ref-J.C3.B3n_R._Hj.C3.A1lmarsson_1993_p._70_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J.C3.B3n_R._Hj.C3.A1lmarsson_1993_p._70-35">[33]</a></sup> Von Mervitz seized a monastery in <a href="/wiki/Vi%C3%B0ey" title="Viðey">Viðey</a> with the help of his sheriff, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dietrich_of_Minden&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dietrich of Minden (page does not exist)">Dietrich of Minden</a>, and his soldiers. They drove the monks out and seized all their possessions, for which they were promptly excommunicated by Ögmundur.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_of_England">Church of England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Church of England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2041" data-file-height="3589" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Workshop_of_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> broke England's ties with the Catholic Church, becoming the sole head of the English Church.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The separation of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> (or Anglican Church) from Rome under <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, beginning in 1529 and completed in 1537, brought England alongside this broad Reformation movement; however, religious changes in the English national church proceeded more conservatively than elsewhere in Europe. Reformers in the Church of England alternated, for decades, between sympathies for ancient Catholic tradition and more Reformed principles, gradually developing, within the context of robustly Protestant doctrine, a tradition considered a middle way (<i><a href="/wiki/Via_media" title="Via media">via media</a></i>) between the Roman Catholic and Protestant traditions.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> followed a different course from the Reformation in continental Europe. There had long been a strong strain of anti-clericalism. England had already given rise to the <a href="/wiki/Lollard" class="mw-redirect" title="Lollard">Lollard</a> movement of <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>, which played an important part in inspiring the <a href="/wiki/Hussite" class="mw-redirect" title="Hussite">Hussites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>. Lollardy was suppressed and became an underground movement, so the extent of its influence in the 1520s is difficult to assess. The different character of the English Reformation came rather from the fact that it was driven initially by the political necessities of Henry VIII.</p>
<p>Henry had once been a sincere Roman Catholic and had even authored a book strongly criticising Luther. His wife, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>, bore him only a single child that survived infancy, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a>. Henry strongly wanted a male heir, and many of his subjects might have agreed, if only because they wanted to avoid another dynastic conflict like the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/170px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/255px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/340px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3129" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a> proved essential in the development of the English Reformation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>King Henry decided to remove the Church of England from the authority of Rome. In 1534, the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Supremacy">Act of Supremacy</a> recognized Henry as "the only <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Head" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Head">Supreme Head</a> on earth of the Church of England".<sup id="cite_ref-bray113_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bray113-36">[34]</a></sup> Between 1535 and 1540, under <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell,_1st_Earl_of_Essex" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex">Thomas Cromwell</a>, the policy known as the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a> was put into effect. The veneration of some <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, certain pilgrimages and some pilgrim shrines were also attacked. Huge amounts of church land and property passed into the hands of the Crown and ultimately into those of the nobility and gentry. The vested interest thus created made for a powerful force in support of the dissolutions.</p>
<p>There were some notable opponents to the Henrician Reformation, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a> and Bishop <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher" title="John Fisher">John Fisher</a>, who were executed for their opposition. There was also a growing party of reformers who were imbued with the Calvinistic, Lutheran and Zwinglian doctrines now current on the Continent. When Henry died he was succeeded by his Protestant son <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a>, who, through his empowered councillors (with the King being only nine years old at his succession and fifteen at his death) the Duke of Somerset and the Duke of Northumberland, ordered the destruction of images in churches, and the closing of the <a href="/wiki/Chantry" title="Chantry">chantries</a>. Under Edward VI the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> moved closer to continental Protestantism.</p>
<p>Yet, at a popular level, religion in England was still in a state of flux. Following a brief Roman Catholic restoration during the reign of Mary (1553–1558), a loose consensus developed during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>, though this point is one of considerable debate among historians. This "<a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a>" largely formed <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> into a distinctive church tradition. The compromise was uneasy and was capable of veering between extreme <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> on one hand and Roman Catholicism on the other. But compared to the bloody and chaotic state of affairs in contemporary France, it was relatively successful, in part because Queen Elizabeth lived so long, until the Puritan Revolution or <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> in the 17th century.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_of_Wales">Church of Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Church of Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a></div>
<p>Bishop <a href="/wiki/Richard_Davies_(bishop)" title="Richard Davies (bishop)">Richard Davies</a> and dissident Protestant cleric <a href="/wiki/John_Penry" title="John Penry">John Penry</a> introduced Calvinist theology to Wales. In 1588, the Bishop of Llandaff published the entire Bible into the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh language</a>. The translation had a significant impact upon the Welsh population and helped to firmly establish Protestantism among the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> The Welsh Protestants used the model of the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Dort" title="Synod of Dort">Synod of Dort</a> of 1618–1619. Calvinism developed through the Puritan period, following the restoration of the monarchy under Charles II, and within Wales' Methodist movement. However few copies of Calvin's were available before mid 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Puritan_movement">Puritan movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Puritan movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/220px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/330px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/440px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2916" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> was a devout Puritan and military leader, who came to power in the Commonwealth of England, Scotland, and Ireland.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The success of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> on the Continent and the growth of a <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> party dedicated to further Protestant reform polarised the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan Age">Elizabethan Age</a>, although it was not until the 1640s that England underwent religious strife comparable to what its neighbours had suffered some generations before.</p>
<p>The early <i>Puritan movement</i> (late 16th–17th centuries) was <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> or <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> and was a movement for reform in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. Its origins lay in the discontent with the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a>. The desire was for the Church of England to resemble more closely the Protestant churches of Europe, especially <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>. The Puritans objected to ornaments and ritual in the churches as <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatrous</a> (vestments, surplices, organs, genuflection), which they castigated as "<a href="/wiki/Papist" title="Papist">popish</a> pomp and rags". (See <a href="/wiki/Vestments_controversy" title="Vestments controversy">Vestments controversy</a>.) They also objected to ecclesiastical courts. Their refusal to endorse completely all of the ritual directions and formulas of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i> and the imposition of its liturgical order by legal force and inspection sharpened Puritanism into a definite opposition movement.</p>
<p>The later Puritan movement, often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dissenters" class="mw-redirect" title="Dissenters">dissenters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconformists</a>, eventually led to the formation of various <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">reformed</a> <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denominations</a>.</p>
<p>The most famous emigration to America was the migration of Puritan separatists from the Anglican Church of England. They fled first to Holland, and then later to America to establish the English <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">colony of Massachusetts</a> in New England, which later became one of the original United States. These Puritan separatists were also known as "the <a href="/wiki/Pilgrim_Fathers" class="mw-redirect" title="Pilgrim Fathers">Pilgrims</a>". After establishing a colony at <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Colony" title="Plymouth Colony">Plymouth</a> (which became part of the colony of Massachusetts) in 1620, the Puritan pilgrims received a charter from the <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a> that legitimised their colony, allowing them to do trade and commerce with merchants in England, in accordance with the principles of <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilism</a>. The Puritans persecuted those of other religious faiths.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Mary_Dyer" title="Mary Dyer">Mary Dyer</a> was hanged in Boston for repeatedly defying a Puritan law banning Quakers from the colony.<sup id="cite_ref-PER_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PER-40">[38]</a></sup> She was one of the four executed Quakers known as the <a href="/wiki/Boston_martyrs" title="Boston martyrs">Boston martyrs</a>. Executions ceased in 1661 when <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King Charles II</a> explicitly forbade Massachusetts from executing anyone for professing Quakerism.<sup id="cite_ref-CHLS_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHLS-41">[39]</a></sup> In 1647, Massachusetts passed a law prohibiting any <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit Roman Catholic</a> priests from entering territory under Puritan jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> Any suspected person who could not clear himself was to be banished from the colony; a second offense carried a death penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Pilgrims held radical Protestant disapproval of <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>, and its celebration was outlawed in Boston from 1659 to 1681.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnett_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-44">[42]</a></sup> The ban was revoked in 1681 by the English-appointed governor <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Andros" title="Edmund Andros">Edmund Andros</a>, who also revoked a Puritan ban on festivities on Saturday nights.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnett_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-44">[42]</a></sup> Nevertheless, it was not until the mid-19th century that celebrating Christmas became fashionable in the Boston region.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation" title="Scottish Reformation">Scottish Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Knox_preaching.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/John_Knox_preaching.JPG/220px-John_Knox_preaching.JPG" width="220" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/John_Knox_preaching.JPG/330px-John_Knox_preaching.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/John_Knox_preaching.JPG/440px-John_Knox_preaching.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1896" data-file-height="1837" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Knox_preaching.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> was a leading figure in the Scottish Reformation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Reformation in Scotland's case culminated ecclesiastically in the establishment of a church along <a href="/wiki/Reformed_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed theology">reformed</a> lines, and politically in the triumph of English influence over that of France. <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> is regarded as the leader of the Scottish reformation.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation_Parliament" title="Scottish Reformation Parliament">Reformation Parliament</a> of 1560 repudiated the pope's authority by the <a href="/wiki/Papal_Jurisdiction_Act_1560" title="Papal Jurisdiction Act 1560">Papal Jurisdiction Act 1560</a>, forbade the celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a> and approved a <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Confession_of_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Confession of Faith">Confession of Faith</a>. It was made possible by a revolution against French hegemony under the regime of the <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Guise" title="Mary of Guise">Mary of Guise</a>, who had governed Scotland in the name of her absent daughter <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a> (then also <a href="/wiki/Queen_consort" title="Queen consort">Queen</a> of France).</p>
<p>Although Protestantism triumphed relatively easily in Scotland, the exact form of Protestantism remained to be determined. The 17th century saw a complex struggle between <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a> (particularly the <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Episcopalianism</a>. The Presbyterians eventually won control of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, which went on to have an important influence on Presbyterian churches worldwide, but Scotland retained a relatively large <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Episcopal_Church" title="Scottish Episcopal Church">Episcopalian minority</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
