<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Church_of_France" title="Reformed Church of France">Reformed Church of France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">French Wars of Religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richelieu,_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_(detalle).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg/220px-Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg/330px-Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg/440px-Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="849" data-file-height="702" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richelieu,_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_(detalle).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Although a Roman Catholic clergyman himself, <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Richelieu" title="Cardinal Richelieu">Cardinal Richelieu</a> allied France with Protestant states.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Protestantism also spread from the German lands into France, where the Protestants were nicknamed <i><a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a></i>; this eventually led to decades of civil warfare.</p>
<p>Though not personally interested in religious reform, <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a> (reigned 1515–1547) initially maintained an attitude of tolerance, in accordance with his interest in the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Humanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance Humanism">humanist</a> movement. This changed in 1534 with the <a href="/wiki/Affair_of_the_Placards" title="Affair of the Placards">Affair of the Placards</a>. In this act, Protestants denounced the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Mass">Catholic Mass</a> in placards that appeared across France, even reaching the royal apartments. The issue of religious faith having been thrown into the arena of politics, Francis came to view the movement as a threat to the kingdom's stability. This led to the first major phase of anti-Protestant persecution in France, in which the <i><a href="/wiki/Chambre_Ardente" title="Chambre Ardente">Chambre Ardente</a></i> ("Burning Chamber") was established (1535) within the <a href="/wiki/Parlement_of_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Parlement of Paris">Parlement of Paris</a> to deal with the rise in prosecutions for heresy. Several thousand French Protestants fled the country, most notably <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>, who emigrated to Basel in 1535 before eventually settling in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> in 1536.</p>
<p>Calvin continued to take an interest in the religious affairs of his native land and, from his base in Geneva, beyond the reach of the French kings, regularly trained pastors to lead congregations in France. Despite heavy persecution by King <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_France" title="Henry II of France">Henry II of France</a> (reigned 1547–1559), the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Church_of_France" title="Reformed Church of France">Reformed Church of France</a>, largely <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> in direction, made steady progress across large sections of the nation, in the urban <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>, appealing to people alienated by the obduracy and the complacency of the Catholic establishment.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francois_Dubois_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Francois_Dubois_001.jpg/220px-Francois_Dubois_001.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Francois_Dubois_001.jpg/330px-Francois_Dubois_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Francois_Dubois_001.jpg/440px-Francois_Dubois_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1331" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francois_Dubois_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Saint_Bartholomew%27s_Day_massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Bartholomew's Day massacre">Saint Bartholomew's Day massacre</a>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois" title="François Dubois">François Dubois</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>French Protestantism, though its appeal increased under persecution, came to acquire a distinctly political character, made all the more obvious by the conversions of nobles during the 1550s. This established the preconditions for a series of destructive and intermittent conflicts, known as the <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">Wars of Religion</a>. The civil wars gained impetus with the sudden death of <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_France" title="Henry II of France">Henry II</a> in 1559, which began a prolonged period of weakness for the French crown. <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/atrocity" class="extiw" title="wikt:atrocity">Atrocity</a> and outrage became the defining characteristics of the time, illustrated at their most intense in the <a href="/wiki/St._Bartholomew%27s_Day_massacre" title="St. Bartholomew's Day massacre">St. Bartholomew's Day massacre</a> of August 1572, when the Roman Catholic party annihilated between 30,000 and 100,000 Huguenots across France. The wars only concluded when <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV</a>, himself a former Huguenot, issued the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Nantes" title="Edict of Nantes">Edict of Nantes</a> (1598), promising official toleration of the Protestant minority, but under highly restricted conditions. Roman Catholicism remained the official state religion, and the fortunes of French Protestants gradually declined over the next century, culminating in Louis XIV's <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Fontainebleau" title="Edict of Fontainebleau">Edict of Fontainebleau</a> (1685), which revoked the Edict of Nantes and made Roman Catholicism the sole legal religion of France. In response to the Edict of Fontainebleau, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_I,_Elector_of_Brandenburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick William I, Elector of Brandenburg">Frederick William I, Elector of Brandenburg</a> declared the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Potsdam" title="Edict of Potsdam">Edict of Potsdam</a> (October 1685), giving free passage to Huguenot refugees and tax-free status to them for ten years.</p>
<p>In the late 17th century many Huguenots fled to England, the Netherlands, Prussia, Switzerland, and the English and Dutch overseas colonies. A significant community in France remained in the <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9vennes" title="Cévennes">Cévennes</a> region. A separate Protestant community, of the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> faith, existed in the newly conquered (1639– ) province of <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a>, its status not affected by the Edict of Fontainebleau.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg/170px-Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg" width="170" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg/255px-Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg/340px-Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1085" data-file-height="1845" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The New Testament translated by Enzinas, published in <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> (1543)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spain had a different political and cultural milieu from its Western and Central European neighbors in several aspects during the early 16th century, and these unique aspects affected the mentality and the reaction of the nation towards the Protestant Reformation. Spain, which had only recently managed to complete the reconquest of the Peninsula from the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> in 1492, had been preoccupied with converting the Muslim and Jewish population of the newly conquered regions through the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Spanish Inquisition</a> in 1478. The rulers of the nation stressed political, cultural, and religious unity, and by the time of the Lutheran Reformation the Spanish Inquisition was already 40 years old and had the capability of quickly dealing with any new movement that the Catholic Church perceived or interpreted to be religious heterodoxy.<sup id="cite_ref-Pettegree304_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pettegree304-46">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Charles_V" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Charles V">Charles V</a> did not wish to see Spain or the rest of Habsburg Europe divided, and in light of continual threat from the Ottomans, preferred to see the Catholic Church reform itself from within. This led to a <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> in Spain in the 1530s. During the 1520s, the Spanish Inquisition had created an atmosphere of suspicion and sought to root out any religious thought seen as suspicious. As early as 1521, the Pope had written a letter to the Spanish monarchy warning against allowing the unrest in Northern Europe to be repeated in Spain. Between 1520 and 1550, printing presses in Spain were tightly controlled, and any books of Protestant teaching were prohibited.</p>
<p>Between 1530 and 1540 Protestantism in Spain was still able to gain followers clandestinely, and in cities such as <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valladolid" title="Valladolid">Valladolid</a> adherents would secretly meet at private houses to pray and study the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-Estep299_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Estep299-47">[45]</a></sup> Protestants in Spain were estimated at between 1000 and 3000, mainly among intellectuals who had seen writings such as those of <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>. Notable reformers included Dr. Juan Gil and Juan Pérez de Pineda who subsequently fled and worked alongside others such as <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Enzinas" title="Francisco de Enzinas">Francisco de Enzinas</a> to translate the Greek <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> into the Spanish language, a task completed by 1556. Protestant teachings were smuggled into Spain by Spaniards such as Julián Hernández, who in 1557 was condemned by the Inquisition and burnt at the stake. Under <a href="/wiki/Phillip_II_of_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Phillip II of Spain">Philip II</a> conservatives in the Spanish church tightened their grip, and those who refused to recant such as Rodrigo de Valer were condemned to life imprisonment. In May 1559, sixteen Spanish Lutherans were burnt at the stake: fourteen were strangled before being burnt, two were burnt alive. In October another thirty were executed. Spanish Protestants who were able to flee the country were to be found in at least a dozen cities in Europe such as <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, where some of them embraced <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> teachings. Those who fled to England were given support by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Kingdom of Navarre</a>, now mostly a part of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> had <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> monarchs including <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV of France</a> and his mother, <a href="/wiki/Jeanne_III_of_Navarre" class="mw-redirect" title="Jeanne III of Navarre">Jeanne III of Navarre</a>, a devout Calvinist.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Reformation did not succeed in Portugal, as similar reasons to Spain prevented it from spreading.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion_in_the_Netherlands" title="History of religion in the Netherlands">History of religion in the Netherlands</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holbein-erasmus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/170px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/255px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/340px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="809" data-file-height="1145" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holbein-erasmus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> was a Catholic priest who inspired some of the Protestant reformers.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Reformation in the Netherlands, unlike in many other countries, was not initiated by the rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Seventeen_Provinces" title="Seventeen Provinces">Seventeen Provinces</a>, but instead by multiple popular movements, which in turn were bolstered by the arrival of Protestant refugees from other parts of the continent. While the <a href="/wiki/Anabaptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a> movement enjoyed popularity in the region in the early decades of the Reformation, Calvinism, in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Dutch Reformed Church</a>, became the dominant Protestant faith in the country from the 1560s onward.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Harsh <a href="/wiki/Inquisition_of_the_Netherlands" title="Inquisition of the Netherlands">persecution</a> of Protestants by the Spanish government of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II</a> contributed to a desire for independence in the provinces, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Eighty_Years%27_War" title="Eighty Years' War">Eighty Years' War</a> and, eventually, the separation of the largely Protestant <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> from the Roman Catholic-dominated <a href="/wiki/Southern_Netherlands" title="Southern Netherlands">Southern Netherlands</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Luxembourg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, a part of the Spanish Netherlands, remained Roman Catholic.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Hungary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg/170px-4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg/255px-4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg/340px-4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1242" data-file-height="1533" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:4k_ref_portre_bocskai.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Stephen_Bocskay" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen Bocskay">Stephen Bocskay</a> prevented the Holy Roman Emperor from imposing Roman Catholicism on Hungarians.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Much of the population of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a> adopted Protestantism during the 16th century. After the 1526 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Moh%C3%A1cs" title="Battle of Mohács">Battle of Mohács</a>, the Hungarian people were disillusioned by the ability of the government to protect them and turned to the faith they felt would infuse them with the strength necessary to resist the invader. They found this in the teaching of the Protestant reformers such as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>. The spread of Protestantism in the country was aided by its large ethnic German minority, which could understand and translate the <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_(resources)" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther (resources)">writings of Martin Luther</a>. While <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> gained a foothold among the German- and Slovak-speaking populations, <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> became widely accepted among ethnic Hungarians.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tranoscius.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tranoscius.JPG/170px-Tranoscius.JPG" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tranoscius.JPG/255px-Tranoscius.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tranoscius.JPG/340px-Tranoscius.JPG 2x" data-file-width="412" data-file-height="570" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tranoscius.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ji%C5%99%C3%AD_T%C5%99anovsk%C3%BD" title="Jiří Třanovský">Jiří Třanovský</a> (1592–1637), the "<a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Luther</a> of the Slavs" who was active in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the more independent northwest the rulers and priests, protected now by the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Habsburg Monarchy</a>, which had taken the field to fight the Turks, defended the old Roman Catholic faith. They dragged the Protestants to prison and the stake wherever they could. Such strong measures only fanned the flames of protest, however. Leaders of the Protestants included Matthias Biro Devai, Michael Sztarai, and Stephen Kis Szegedi.</p>
<p>Protestants likely formed a majority of Hungary's population at the close of the 16th century, but <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> efforts in the 17th century reconverted a majority of the kingdom to Roman Catholicism. A significant Protestant minority remained, most of it adhering to the Calvinist faith.</p>
<p>In 1558 the <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvanian</a> <a href="/wiki/Diet_(assembly)" title="Diet (assembly)">Diet</a> of <a href="/wiki/Turda" title="Turda">Turda</a> declared free practice of both the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> religions, but prohibited <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>. Ten years later, in 1568, the Diet extended this freedom, declaring that "It is not allowed to anybody to intimidate anybody with captivity or expelling for his religion". Four religions (<a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarianism</a> became official in 1583, following the faith of the only Unitarian King <a href="/wiki/John_II_Sigismund_Z%C3%A1polya" class="mw-redirect" title="John II Sigismund Zápolya">John II Sigismund Zápolya</a> 1541–1571) were declared as accepted (recepta) religions, while <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christianity</a> was "tolerated" (though the building of stone Orthodox churches was forbidden). During the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>, Royal (Habsburg) Hungary joined the Roman Catholic side, until Transylvania joined the Protestant side.</p>
<p>There were a series of other successful and unsuccessful anti-Habsburg (requiring equal rights and freedom for all Christian denominations) uprisings between 1604 and 1711; the uprisings were usually organised from Transylvania. The constrained Habsburg Counter-Reformation efforts in the 17th century reconverted the majority of the kingdom to Roman Catholicism.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Ireland" title="Reformation in Ireland">Reformation in Ireland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maria_Tudor1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Maria_Tudor1.jpg/170px-Maria_Tudor1.jpg" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Maria_Tudor1.jpg/255px-Maria_Tudor1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Maria_Tudor1.jpg/340px-Maria_Tudor1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2305" data-file-height="3051" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maria_Tudor1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A devout Roman Catholic, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I of England</a> started the first <a href="/wiki/Plantations_of_Ireland" title="Plantations of Ireland">Plantations of Ireland</a>, which, ironically, soon came to be associated with Protestantism.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Reformation in Ireland was a movement for the reform of religious life and institutions that was introduced into Ireland by the English administration at the behest of King Henry VIII of England. His desire for an annulment of his marriage was known as the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Great_Matter" class="mw-redirect" title="King's Great Matter">King's Great Matter</a>. Ultimately <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VII" title="Pope Clement VII">Pope Clement VII</a> refused the petition; consequently it became necessary for the King to assert his lordship over the Roman Catholic Church in his realm to give legal effect to his wishes. The <a href="/wiki/English_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="English Parliament">English Parliament</a> confirmed the King's supremacy over the Church in the Kingdom of England. This challenge to <a href="/wiki/Papal_supremacy" title="Papal supremacy">Papal supremacy</a> resulted in a breach with the Roman Catholic Church. By 1541, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Irish Parliament</a> had agreed to the change in status of the country from that of a <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship</a> to that of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a>.</p>
<p>Unlike similar movements for religious reform on the continent of Europe, the various phases of the English Reformation as it developed in Ireland were largely driven by changes in government policy, to which public opinion in England gradually accommodated itself. However, a number of factors complicated the adoption of the religious innovations in Ireland; the majority of the population there adhered to the Roman Catholic Church. However, in the city of <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> the reformation took hold under the auspices of <a href="/wiki/George_Browne_(Archbishop_of_Dublin)" class="mw-redirect" title="George Browne (Archbishop of Dublin)">George Browne</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Italy" title="Reformation in Italy">Reformation in Italy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldenser-Wappen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Waldenser-Wappen.jpg/170px-Waldenser-Wappen.jpg" width="170" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Waldenser-Wappen.jpg/255px-Waldenser-Wappen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Waldenser-Wappen.jpg/340px-Waldenser-Wappen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="465" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldenser-Wappen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Waldensian symbol <i>Lux lucet in tenebris</i> ("Light glows in the darkness")</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Word of the Protestant reformers reached Italy in the 1520s but never caught on. Its development was stopped by the Counter-Reformation, the Inquisition and also popular disinterest. Not only was the Church highly aggressive in seeking out and suppressing heresy, but there was a shortage of Protestant leadership. No one translated the Bible into Italian; few tracts were written. No core of Protestantism emerged. The few preachers who did take an interest in "Lutheranism," as it was called in Italy, were suppressed or went into exile to northern countries where their message was well received. As a result, the Reformation exerted almost no lasting influence in Italy, except for strengthening the Roman Catholic Church and motivating the Counter-Reformation.<sup id="cite_ref-McCulloch401_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCulloch401-48">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Firp169_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firp169-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some Protestants left Italy and became outstanding activists of the European Reformation, mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Biandrata" title="Giorgio Biandrata">Giorgio Biandrata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernardino_Ochino" title="Bernardino Ochino">Bernardino Ochino</a>, Giovanni Alciato, Giovanni Battista Cetis, <a href="/wiki/Fausto_Sozzini" title="Fausto Sozzini">Fausto Sozzini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Stancaro" title="Francesco Stancaro">Francesco Stancaro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Valentino_Gentile" title="Giovanni Valentino Gentile">Giovanni Valentino Gentile</a>), who propagated <a href="/wiki/Nontrinitarianism" title="Nontrinitarianism">Nontrinitarianism</a> there and were chief instigators of the movement of <a href="/wiki/Polish_Brethren" title="Polish Brethren">Polish Brethren</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Church457_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Church457-50">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1532 the <a href="/wiki/Waldensians" title="Waldensians">Waldensians</a> adhered to the Reformation, adopting the Calvinist theology. The <a href="/wiki/Waldensian_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Waldensian Church">Waldensian Church</a> survived in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Alps" title="Western Alps">Western Alps</a> through many persecutions and remains a Protestant church in Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-Wald_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wald-51">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland_and_Lithuania">Poland and Lithuania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Poland and Lithuania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformation in Poland">Reformation in Poland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG/170px-Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG/255px-Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG/340px-Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1166" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_a_Lasco.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jan_%C5%81aski" title="Jan Łaski">Jan Łaski</a> sought unity between various Christian churches in the Commonwealth, and participated in the English Reformation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the first half of the 16th century, the enormous <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> was a country of many creeds, but Roman Catholic Church remained the dominating religion. Reformation reached Poland in the 1520s and quickly gained popularity among mostly German-speaking inhabitants of such major cities as <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toruń">Toruń</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elbl%C4%85g" title="Elbląg">Elbląg</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Koenigsberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Koenigsberg">Koenigsberg</a>, in 1530, a Polish-language edition of <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Small_Catechism" title="Luther's Small Catechism">Luther's Small Catechism</a> was published. The <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Prussia" title="Duchy of Prussia">Duchy of Prussia</a>, which was a Polish <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fief</a>, emerged as a key center of the movement, with numerous publishing houses issuing not only Bibles, but also catechisms, in German, Polish and Lithuanian.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> gained popularity in the northern part of the country, while <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> caught the interest of the nobility (known as <a href="/wiki/Szlachta" title="Szlachta">szlachta</a>), mainly in <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Poland" title="Lesser Poland">Lesser Poland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a>. Several publishing houses were opened in Lesser Poland in the mid-16th century in such locations as <a href="/wiki/S%C5%82omniki" title="Słomniki">Słomniki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rak%C3%B3w,_Kielce_County" title="Raków, Kielce County">Raków</a>. At that time, <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">Mennonites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unity_of_the_Brethren" title="Unity of the Brethren">Czech Brothers</a> came to Poland, with the latter settling mostly in <a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland" title="Greater Poland">Greater Poland</a> around <a href="/wiki/Leszno" title="Leszno">Leszno</a>. In 1565, the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Brethren" title="Polish Brethren">Polish Brethren</a> appeared as yet another reformation movement.</p>
<p>The Commonwealth was unique in Europe in the 16th century for its widespread tolerance confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Confederation" title="Warsaw Confederation">Warsaw Confederation</a>. In 1563, the <a href="/wiki/Brest_Bible" title="Brest Bible">Brest Bible</a> was published (see also <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Polish" title="Bible translations into Polish">Bible translations into Polish</a>). The period of tolerance ended during the reign of King <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_III_Vasa" title="Sigismund III Vasa">Sigismund III Vasa</a>, who was under the strong influence of <a href="/wiki/Piotr_Skarga" title="Piotr Skarga">Piotr Skarga</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuits</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Deluge_(history)" title="Deluge (history)">Deluge</a>, and other wars of the mid-17th century in which all enemies of Poland were either Protestant or Orthodox Christians, the Poles' attitude changed, and the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> prevailed: in 1658 the Polish Brethren were forced to leave the country, and in 1666, the <a href="/wiki/Sejm" title="Sejm">Sejm</a> banned <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a> from Catholicism to any other religion, under punishment of death. Finally, in 1717, the <a href="/wiki/Silent_Sejm" title="Silent Sejm">Silent Sejm</a> banned non-Catholics from becoming deputies of the Parliament.</p>
<p>Among most important Protestants of the Commonwealth were <a href="/wiki/Miko%C5%82aj_Rej" title="Mikołaj Rej">Mikołaj Rej</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcin_Czechowic" title="Marcin Czechowic">Marcin Czechowic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrzej_Frycz_Modrzewski" title="Andrzej Frycz Modrzewski">Andrzej Frycz Modrzewski</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symon_Budny" title="Symon Budny">Symon Budny</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slovenia">Slovenia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Slovenia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Primoz-Trubar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Primoz-Trubar.jpg/170px-Primoz-Trubar.jpg" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Primoz-Trubar.jpg/255px-Primoz-Trubar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Primoz-Trubar.jpg/340px-Primoz-Trubar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2623" data-file-height="3971" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Primoz-Trubar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Primo%C5%BE_Trubar" title="Primož Trubar">Primož Trubar</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Primo%C5%BE_Trubar" title="Primož Trubar">Primož Trubar</a> is notable for consolidating the <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">Slovene language</a> and is considered to be the key figure of Slovenian cultural history and in many aspects a major Slovene historical personality.<sup id="cite_ref-Voglar_I_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voglar_I-52">[50]</a></sup> He was the key figure of the Protestant Church of the <a href="/wiki/Slovene_Lands" title="Slovene Lands">Slovene Lands</a>, as he was its founder and its first superintendent. The first books in Slovene, <i><a href="/wiki/Catechismus" title="Catechismus">Catechismus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Abecedarium_(Trubar)" title="Abecedarium (Trubar)">Abecedarium</a></i>, were written by Trubar.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Croatia">Croatia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Croatia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> reached northern parts of the country.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serbia">Serbia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Serbia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vojvodina" title="Vojvodina">Vojvodina</a> turned partially <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Protestant teachings of the Western Church were also briefly adopted within the Eastern Orthodox Church through the <a href="/wiki/Greek_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek people">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarch of Constantinople">Patriarch</a> <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Lucaris" title="Cyril Lucaris">Cyril Lucaris</a> in 1629 with the publishing of the <i>Confessio</i> (Calvinistic doctrine) in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>. Motivating factors in their decision to adopt aspects of the Reformation included the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">historical rivalry</a> and mistrust between the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a> and Roman Catholic church along with their concerns of <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> priests entering Greek lands in their attempts to propagate the teachings of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> to the Greek populace. He subsequently sponsored <a href="/wiki/Maximos_of_Gallipoli" title="Maximos of Gallipoli">Maximos of Gallipoli</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Greek" title="Bible translations into Greek">translation of the New Testament</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern Greek language">Modern Greek language</a> and was published in Geneva in 1638. Upon Lucaris's death in 1638, the conservative factions within the Eastern Orthodox Church held two synods: the Synod of Constantinople (1638) and <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jassy" title="Synod of Jassy">Synod of Jassy</a> (1642) criticizing the reforms and in the 1672 convocation led by <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Dositheos_II_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarch Dositheos II of Jerusalem">Dositheos</a>, they officially condemned the Calvinistic doctrines.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Spread">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Protestant_Reformation.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/The_Protestant_Reformation.svg/800px-The_Protestant_Reformation.svg.png" width="800" height="673" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/The_Protestant_Reformation.svg/1200px-The_Protestant_Reformation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/The_Protestant_Reformation.svg/1600px-The_Protestant_Reformation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2045" data-file-height="1720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Protestant_Reformation.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Protestant Reformation at its peak, superimposed on modern European borders</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conclusion_and_legacy">Conclusion and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Conclusion and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_Reformation">End of the Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: End of the Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no universal agreement on the exact or approximate date the Protestant Reformation ended. Various interpretations emphasize different dates, entire periods, or argue that the Reformation never really ended. However, there are a few popular interpretations that are used by large groups of observers.</p>
<ul>
<li>Historically, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> is considered to be the event that ended the Reformation. This is the most commonly held interpretation;</li>
<li>According to other historical interpretation, the Reformation could truly be considered to have ended in the middle 18th century, as the Peace of Westphalia did not specify, nor did it mean that it concluded; that is around time the <a href="/wiki/First_Great_Awakening" title="First Great Awakening">First Great Awakening</a> (1730–1755) took place. People who hold this interpretation often argue that the emergence of Pietism prolonged the Reformation up to this point;</li>
<li>Some argue that the Reformation never ended as new groups have splintered from the Catholic Church (e.g., Old Catholics, Polish National Catholic Church, etc.), as well as all the various Protestant churches that exist today. No group splintering from the Catholic Church since the 17th century has done so on the basis of the same issues animating the Protestant Reformation, however.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span id="Thirty_Years'_War:_1618–1648"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thirty_Years.27_War:_1618.E2.80.931648">Thirty Years' War: 1618–1648</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Thirty Years' War: 1618–1648">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_(Gerard_Terborch_1648).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_%28Gerard_Terborch_1648%29.jpg/250px-Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_%28Gerard_Terborch_1648%29.jpg" width="250" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_%28Gerard_Terborch_1648%29.jpg/375px-Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_%28Gerard_Terborch_1648%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_%28Gerard_Terborch_1648%29.jpg/500px-Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_%28Gerard_Terborch_1648%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5456" data-file-height="4225" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westfaelischer_Friede_in_Muenster_(Gerard_Terborch_1648).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Westphalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Westphalia">Treaty of Westphalia</a> allowed <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> to be freely exercised</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Reformation led to a <a href="/wiki/European_wars_of_religion" title="European wars of religion">series of religious wars</a> that culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> (1618–1648), which devastated much of <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_history_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern history of Germany">Germany</a>, killing between 25% and 40% of its entire population.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">House of Habsburg</a> and its allies fought against the Protestant princes of Germany, supported at various times by Denmark, Sweden and France. The Habsburgs, who ruled Spain, Austria, the <a href="/wiki/Lands_of_the_Bohemian_Crown" title="Lands of the Bohemian Crown">Crown of Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovene_Lands" title="Slovene Lands">Slovene Lands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Netherlands" title="Spanish Netherlands">Spanish Netherlands</a> and much of Germany and Italy, were staunch defenders of the Roman Catholic Church. Some historians believe that the era of the Reformation came to a close when Roman Catholic France allied itself with Protestant states against the Habsburg dynasty. For the first time since the days of Martin Luther, political and national convictions again outweighed religious convictions in Europe.</p>
<p>Two main tenets of the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>, which ended the Thirty Years' War, were:</p>
<ul>
<li>All parties would now recognise the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Augsburg" title="Peace of Augsburg">Peace of Augsburg</a> of 1555, by which each prince would have the right to determine the religion of his own state, the options being Roman Catholicism, Lutheranism, and now Calvinism (the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Cuius_regio,_eius_religio" title="Cuius regio, eius religio">cuius regio, eius religio</a></i>).</li>
<li>Christians living in principalities where their denomination was <i>not</i> the established church were guaranteed the right to practice their faith in public during allotted hours and in private at their will.</li>
</ul>
<p>The treaty also effectively ended the Papacy's pan-European political power. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_X" title="Pope Innocent X">Pope Innocent X</a> declared the treaty "null, void, invalid, iniquitous, unjust, damnable, reprobate, inane, empty of meaning and effect for all times" in his bull <i>Zelo Domus Dei</i>. European sovereigns, Roman Catholic and Protestant alike, ignored his verdict.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCCWestphalia_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCCWestphalia-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences_of_the_Protestant_Reformation">Consequences of the Protestant Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Consequences of the Protestant Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following outcomes of the Protestant Reformation regarding <a href="/wiki/Human_capital" title="Human capital">human capital</a> formation, the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">Protestant ethic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Governance" title="Governance">governance</a>, and "dark" outcomes have been identified by scholars:<sup id="cite_ref-:0_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_capital_formation">Human capital formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Human capital formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>Higher literacy rates.<sup id="cite_ref-Becker_531.E2.80.93596_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker_531.E2.80.93596-56">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lower gender gap in school enrollment and literacy rates.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>Higher primary school enrollment.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>Higher public spending on schooling and better educational performance of military conscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>Higher capability in reading, numeracy, essay writing, and history.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_ethic">Protestant ethic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Protestant ethic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>More hours worked.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li>Divergent work attitudes of Protestant and Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup></li>
<li>Fewer referenda on leisure, state intervention, and redistribution in Swiss cantons with more Protestants.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lower life satisfaction when unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>Pro-market attitudes.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup></li>
<li>Income differences between Protestants and Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-Becker_531.E2.80.93596_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker_531.E2.80.93596-56">[54]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_development">Economic development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Economic development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>Different levels of income tax revenue per capita,&#160;% of labor force in manufacturing and services, and incomes of male elementary school teachers.<sup id="cite_ref-Becker_531.E2.80.93596_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker_531.E2.80.93596-56">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Protestant cities grew more.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup></li>
<li>Greater entrepreneurship among religious minorities in Protestant states.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>Different social ethics.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>The Reformation has been credited as a key factor in the development of the state system.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Reformation has been credited as a key factor in the formation of transnational advocacy movements.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Reformation impacted the Western legal tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>Establishment of State churches.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>Poor relief and social welfare regimes.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative_outcomes">Negative outcomes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Negative outcomes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>Witch trials became more common in areas where Protestants and Catholics contested the religious market.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>Protestants were far more likely to vote for Nazis than their Catholic German counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> Christopher J. Probst, in his book <i>Demonizing the Jews: Luther and the Protestant Church in Nazi Germany</i> (2012), shows that a large number of German Lutheran clergy and theologians during the Nazi Third Reich used Luther's hostile publications towards the Jews and Judaism to justify at least in part the anti-Semitic policies of the National Socialists.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>Higher suicide rate and greater suicide acceptability.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Margaret C. Jacob argues that there has been a dramatic shift in the historiography of the Reformation. Until the 1960s, historians focused their attention largely on the great leaders and theologians of the 16th century, especially Luther, Calvin, and Zwingli. Their ideas were studied in depth. However, the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">new social history</a> in the 1960s look at history from the bottom up, not from the top down. Historians began to concentrate on the values, beliefs and behavior of the people at large. She finds, "in contemporary scholarship, the Reformation is now seen as a vast cultural upheaval, a social and popular movement, textured and rich because of its diversity."<sup id="cite_ref-Jacob215_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacob215-83">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anti-Catholicism" title="Anti-Catholicism">Anti-Catholicism</a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the end, while the Reformation emphasis on Protestants reading the Scriptures was one factor in the development of literacy, the impact of printing itself, the wider availability of printed works at a cheaper price, and the increasing focus on education and learning as key factors in obtaining a lucrative post, were also significant contributory factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Pettegree543_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pettegree543-18">[18]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the first decade of the Reformation, Luther's message became a movement, and the output of religious pamphlets in Germany was at its height.<sup id="cite_ref-PettegreeHall786_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PettegreeHall786-22">[21]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>

<th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/Resources_about_Martin_Luther" title="Resources about Martin Luther">Works</a><br />

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Eyn_geystlich_Gesangk_Buchleyn" title="Eyn geystlich Gesangk Buchleyn">Eyn geystlich Gesangk Buchleyn</a></i> (1524</li>
</ul>
